201509	0	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Good afternoon, everyone.	5	5
201509	1	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	I would like to welcome Rob Kaplan to his first FOMC meeting.	5	5
201509	2	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Rob became president and CEO of the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas just last week.	5	5
201509	3	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	In his new role, President Kaplan brings to bear 10 years of academic experience at Harvard University, where he served as a professor and a dean; a long and distinguished career in global finance; and broad experience on corporate and noncorporate boards.	5	5
201509	4	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	I have to say, you chose to join us at a very interesting time.	5	5
201509	5	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	[Laughter]	5	5
201509	6	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	We all look forward to working with you.	5	5
201509	7	2015-09-17	meeting	KAPLAN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	5	5
201509	8	2015-09-17	meeting	KAPLAN	I appreciate it.	5	5
201509	9	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Let me mention, as was indicated in the agenda, that the entire FOMC meeting will be conducted jointly as an FOMC and Board of Governors meeting—and this will likely be true going forward.	5	5
201509	10	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	I need a motion to close the Board meeting.	5	5
201509	11	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	So moved.	5	5
201509	12	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	5	5
201509	13	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	And without objection.	5	5
201509	14	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Our first item of business is “Financial Developments and Open Market Operations.”	61	6
201509	15	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Let me call on Simon to give us the Desk report.	61	6
201509	16	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201509	17	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Over the intermeeting period, a sharp rise in concern about emerging market growth, particularly in China, triggered a broad decline in global risk-sensitive assets.	61	6
201509	18	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Oil and other commodity prices fell, at times reaching levels last observed in early 2009, and inflation compensation measures in the United States and other advanced economies continued to decline.	61	6
201509	19	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Most emerging market currencies depreciated, in some cases quite sharply.	61	6
201509	20	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Nominal Treasury yields were relatively little changed, reportedly in part because flight-to- quality demand was offset by large-scale reserve sales by emerging market central banks, which intervened heavily in foreign exchange markets in a bid to stabilize the value of their currencies.	61	6
201509	21	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Toward the end of the period, as US markets calmed down, investors were intensely focused on the prospects for Federal Reserve policy firming in light of the positive news on US real activity during the intermeeting period.	61	6
201509	22	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	I will begin by discussing these financial market developments.	61	6
201509	23	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Lorie will then discuss money market developments and operational matters.	61	6
201509	24	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The People’s Bank of China unexpectedly devalued the renminbi versus the dollar following a long period of stability and predictability in the management of that exchange rate, as shown in the top-left panel of your first exhibit.	61	6
201509	25	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Although the devaluation was relatively small compared with the increase in China’s trade- weighted exchange rate over the past year, it came on the heels of a string of weak Chinese economic data and the unwinding, at least in part, of a bubble in Chinese equities.	61	6
201509	26	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The devaluation appeared to intensify greatly the existing concerns among market participants about the severity of the growth slowdown in China and the ability of the Chinese authorities to address it appropriately.	61	6
201509	27	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Sentiment toward Chinese policymaking had already been fragile, especially in light of the often heavy- handed and ultimately unsuccessful effort over the past few months to halt the selloff in equities.	61	6
201509	28	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Following the devaluation, capital outflows from China accelerated markedly from already high levels.	61	6
201509	29	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	These outflows led, within a couple of days, to large-scale foreign exchange intervention to prevent further renminbi depreciation.	61	6
201509	30	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The concerns raised by the devaluation, along with a number of significant developments in other emerging markets that I will discuss later, contributed to a broad selloff in global risk assets.	61	6
201509	31	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the top-right panel, equity price indexes in both emerging and developed economies declined sharply.	61	6
201509	32	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The S&P 500 index fell by more than 10 percent over a two-day period before recovering somewhat and is about 7 percent lower, on net, over the period.	61	6
201509	33	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Nominal Treasury yields changed only modestly over the period, and real yields actually increased somewhat at most tenors.	61	6
201509	34	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The middle-left panel shows a decomposition of the changes in forward nominal rates into real and inflation compensation components.	61	6
201509	35	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Some market participants expressed surprise that nominal yields did not fall substantially and found the rise in real yields incongruous with the decline in global risk sentiment and flight-to-quality effects that are ordinarily expected to push yields down in such circumstances.	61	6
201509	36	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants believe that this relative stability in nominal yields reflects the downward pressure of typical flight-to-quality flows being offset by upward pressure stemming from sizable sales of Treasuries by Chinese and other emerging market reserve managers intervening to support their currencies.	61	6
201509	37	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Your middle-right panel presents information about Chinese intervention activity.	61	6
201509	38	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	PBOC official reserve data for August showed a decline in reserve holdings of about $100 billion.	61	6
201509	39	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants believe that this figure might understate the scale of Chinese sales—for example, by omitting intervention via foreign exchange derivatives—and estimate that the PBOC’s intervention might have been up to $200 billion last month.	61	6
201509	40	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Contacts believe that the PBOC funded outright interventions in large part via sales of shorter-dated, off-the-run nominal Treasury securities, and, as Lorie will discuss later, primary dealers’ inventories of these securities rose rapidly.	61	6
201509	41	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition, the PBOC was reportedly active in a range of other assets and across a variety of other currency pairs.	61	6
201509	42	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants generally expect continued high levels of intervention to stem depreciation pressures on the renminbi, with some expecting that intervention could total several hundred billion dollars through the spring of next year.	61	6
201509	43	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The combined effect of these actual and anticipated sales probably will put significant upward pressure on Treasury yields, although it is difficult to quantify the effects.	61	6
201509	44	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Chinese officials appear to be hoping that various recently introduced so-called macroprudential measures will stem capital outflows and reduce the corresponding need for further FX intervention and asset sales to stabilize the currency at around its current level.	61	6
201509	45	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	However, investors appear skeptical that these measures will have much effect on capital flows, which are thought to be motivated by expectations for further renminbi depreciation and a loss of confidence in the Chinese economic outlook.	61	6
201509	46	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	As further evidence of the possible effect of intervention on US Treasuries, market participants have pointed to a narrowing of swap spreads—the difference between interest rate swap rates and cash Treasury yields—shown in the lower-left panel.	61	6
201509	47	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	According to this argument, the narrowing of swap spreads reflects underperformance of Treasuries versus other fixed-income assets in large part due to the concentrated flow of Treasury sales by reserve managers.	61	6
201509	48	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The narrowing has been most notable in short-dated tenors, in which the majority of reserve selling has likely taken place.	61	6
201509	49	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Concern about a slowdown in China and other emerging markets also drove a steep decline in oil and other commodity prices.	61	6
201509	50	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the lower-right panel, oil prices fell 13 percent on net over the period amid very high volatility.	61	6
201509	51	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition to demand-related concerns, the decline in commodity prices also partly reflects expectations for oil production, both by OPEC member countries and by shale- exploration firms, to respond to recent price declines more slowly and to a lesser extent than earlier believed, as well as expectations for a significant rise in oil exports from Iran following the lifting of trade sanctions.	61	6
201509	52	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Declines in commodity prices, the surprise renminbi devaluation, and concern about fragility in many emerging economies have contributed to a significant decrease in emerging market asset prices, most notably in commodity-exporting economies.	61	6
201509	53	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The top-left panel of your next exhibit illustrates the relationship between countries’ intermeeting currency performance, on the vertical axis, against the share of their exports that come from commodities, on the horizontal axis.	61	6
201509	54	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	This panel shows that countries for which energy is a more important export experienced larger currency depreciations; for example, the currencies in the lower-right quadrant depend most on energy exports and exhibited the largest depreciation.	61	6
201509	55	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	These large exchange rate moves appear to reflect concern about the effect of sizable changes in the terms of trade on the economic health and fiscal, financial, political, and social stability of countries whose fortunes are closely tied to commodity exports and to China.	61	6
201509	56	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The performance of the Turkish, Brazilian, Malaysian, and Russian currencies, which lie well below the best-fit line in this figure, also reflects the effects of political developments in these countries as well as, in the case of Brazil, a credit downgrade by Standard & Poor’s to non-investment grade.	61	6
201509	57	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The decline in energy prices has also contributed to a continued fall in inflation compensation at both short and long horizons.	61	6
201509	58	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The top-right panel shows the evolution of 5-year inflation compensation as measured in the swaps market over the past several years in the euro area and the United States.	61	6
201509	59	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Each measure is well below its respective 10-year average, represented by the dashed horizontal lines.	61	6
201509	60	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In the United States, 5-year inflation compensation fell about 25 basis points over the intermeeting period and is now near mid-2010 levels, although market measures of deflation probabilities are lower than they were in that earlier period.	61	6
201509	61	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition to the drop in oil prices, market participants cite low inflation data, the appreciation of the trade-weighted dollar, and concerns about global economic growth as placing downward pressure on US inflation compensation.	61	6
201509	62	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Longer-dated forward inflation compensation also fell over the intermeeting period in the United States and the euro area.	61	6
201509	63	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Respondents to the Desk’s surveys continue to report that their own forward inflation expectations are little changed.	61	6
201509	64	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	At the same time, they attribute about one- third of the decline in long-forward inflation compensation over the period to a decline in the markets’ overall assessment of inflation expectations, which is more than is attributed to this factor by most term structure models used in the System.	61	6
201509	65	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In response to increased concerns about the inflation and growth outlook, the ECB at its September 3 meeting signaled a willingness to change the size, composition, and duration of its purchase program, should conditions warrant, and adjusted one of the key parameters of its program to give more room to enlarge it if necessary.	61	6
201509	66	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Sustained asset price response to this communication was limited.	61	6
201509	67	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	At its meeting this week, the Bank of Japan lowered its expectations for exports and production because of the slowdown in emerging markets but suggested the Japanese economy continues to recover moderately and kept its inflation outlook unchanged.	61	6
201509	68	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	A majority of Japanese market contacts believe the BOJ will expand the scope of its QE program later this year in pursuit of its inflation goal.	61	6
201509	69	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Some contacts report concerns that advanced-economy central banks—many of which are facing persistently low inflation while already at the zero lower bound and using quantitative easing measures—will be unable to adequately react to an external shock stemming from a significant global growth slowdown.	61	6
201509	70	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In a new survey question, we asked the dealers to estimate the probability that the global economy will be in a recession six months from now.	61	6
201509	71	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The average probability was 20 percent, with a range of individual beliefs from 5 to 50 percent.	61	6
201509	72	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	For comparison, the average probability associated with a recession in the United States was 13 percent.	61	6
201509	73	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Separately, market participants report uncertainty about how well some countries, most notably Brazil, will cope with tighter US monetary policy, and note that many countries experienced significant challenges during the 2013 “taper tantrum.”	61	6
201509	74	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	However, it is important to emphasize that most market participants assess that these difficulties in individual countries by themselves are not material to the US outlook.	61	6
201509	75	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	However, with policy firming likely to take place in the United States in the near or very near future, it is useful to recall that one of the lessons of the taper tantrum was that dollar carry trades might be more pervasive than standard measures indicate, and that their unwind can produce a deterioration of financial conditions in the United States through a decline in global risk appetite.	61	6
201509	76	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Thus, it will be important to monitor closely for evidence of carry-trade-type linkages in offshore dollar markets.	61	6
201509	77	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	This is particularly true for activity running through Hong Kong, which is in the unusual position of maintaining a peg to the US dollar while having the most open capital markets with mainland China.	61	6
201509	78	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The rise in uncertainty associated with the global economic outlook prompted an increase in volatility across most assets.	61	6
201509	79	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Your middle-left panel shows the increase in implied volatility, most notably in equities and foreign exchange, in the period since the PBOC’s devaluation.	61	6
201509	80	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Implied volatility for equities in particular remains elevated, although it has come off its highs.	61	6
201509	81	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants reported that liquidity conditions, especially in foreign exchange and equity markets, deteriorated over the period, and that this contributed to some punctuated moves in asset prices, particularly on August 24.	61	6
201509	82	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	As in earlier events, such as the Treasury market moves last October 15, there seemed to be increased intraday volatility when large shocks or belief swings required big position unwinds.	61	6
201509	83	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	In this context, commentary over the period highlighted the effect of position adjustments by so-called risk parity strategies on market volatility.	61	6
201509	84	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	To date, these liquidity events have been transitory and do not seem to have materially affected market pricing.	61	6
201509	85	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	It is possible that these events are just part of the new landscape for financial markets with no implication for policy.	61	6
201509	86	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	While market participants continue to see an increase in the federal funds rate at this meeting as a real possibility, expectations for this declined notably amid the financial market volatility.	61	6
201509	87	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The middle-right panel shows the market-implied probability of liftoff at or before the September meeting, derived from federal funds futures prices and data from the Desk’s surveys about where the effective rate will trade in the target range following liftoff.	61	6
201509	88	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	The probability had been about 50–50 before the PBOC devaluation and is now about 1-in-4 to 1-in-3.	61	6
201509	89	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants have suggested that diverse views expressed in policymaker communications over the period added to uncertainty surrounding the timing of liftoff.	61	6
201509	90	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Thomas will discuss policy expectations and possible market reactions more in his briefing.	61	6
201509	91	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Turning to conditions expected at liftoff, for some time the Desk surveys have asked about expectations for inflation between one and two years ahead at the time of liftoff.	61	6
201509	92	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the lower-left panel, the mean expectation is now that inflation between one and two years after liftoff will run a bit low, at 1.8 percent.	61	6
201509	93	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	This decline appears to reflect a belief that subdued inflation will be somewhat more persistent than earlier thought.	61	6
201509	94	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Let me conclude by noting the tightening in many common components of financial conditions indexes over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201509	95	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Your lower-right panel shows the intermeeting performance of assets that form the core of most such indexes.	61	6
201509	96	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	As the panel shows, conditions in the equity, high-yield credit, foreign exchange, and real interest rate markets all tightened, with mortgages the significant exception.	61	6
201509	97	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	These moves were large both in absolute terms and in comparison with recent historical behavior.	61	6
201509	98	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	This illustrates that adverse developments in China and other emerging market countries have the potential to affect US asset prices in a way that, if sustained, might have significant implications for the US economic outlook.	61	6
201509	99	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	David Wilcox and Steve Kamin will discuss the implications of this risk further in their briefings.	61	6
201509	100	2015-09-17	meeting	POTTER	Lorie will now discuss money markets and operational matters.	61	6
201509	101	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	As Simon noted, measures of domestic financial conditions tightened over the intermeeting period, though this tightening was not accompanied or led by any increase in dollar funding strains.	61	6
201509	102	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	We have, however, observed a relatively modest but steady increase in some money market rates since earlier this year, as shown in the top-left panel of your third exhibit.	61	6
201509	103	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	This trend continued over the period, and the average effective federal funds rate for the month of August, excluding month-end, reached 14 basis points, its highest level in over two years.	61	6
201509	104	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Market participants have speculated that the increase in the effective rate over recent months has been the result of a number of factors, including the pull of higher secured money market rates.	61	6
201509	105	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	In explaining the more recent rise in repo rates, a number of contacts have pointed to foreign official sales to fund the FX intervention that Simon discussed and an associated increase in demand from primary dealers to finance these Treasury securities.	61	6
201509	106	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Indeed, as shown in the top-right panel, primary dealers’ Treasury holdings have increased since early July, leaving them with a larger amount of Treasury collateral to finance.	61	6
201509	107	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	The recent upward pressure arising from these developments is similar to the upward movement in repo rates and the effective rate observed during the maturity extension program, when the Federal Reserve was selling short-dated Treasury securities.	61	6
201509	108	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Amid these higher money market rates, total daily RRP take-up declined somewhat outside month-ends, averaging $78 billion versus $140 billion last period, as shown in the middle-left panel.	61	6
201509	109	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	The pattern of counterparty participation was generally consistent with prior periods, with money market funds continuing to account for most of the participation.	61	6
201509	110	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	The Federal Reserve also continued to test TDF operations over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201509	111	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Take-up at the two overlapping TDF operations in August totaled nearly $125 billion, moderately lower than in the previous operations in May.	61	6
201509	112	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Meanwhile, foreign RP pool participation, shown in grey, was relatively steady over the period.	61	6
201509	113	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	As discussed at the July FOMC meeting, the staff communicated the changes in the repo pool terms of service to FIMA accounts, including the removal of individual target levels and of the requirement that we be notified in advance of changes in daily investment levels in excess of $100 million.	61	6
201509	114	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	The new terms took effect on September 4.	61	6
201509	115	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	We anticipate that the size of the foreign RP pool will grow $50 billion over the next several months based on indications from one large account holder that it plans to increase its use of the service.	61	6
201509	116	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	This institution noted that it plans to increase its liquid investments in anticipation of potential market turbulence over the next year related to US monetary policy normalization.	61	6
201509	117	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Despite the recent upward drift in some money market rates noted earlier, expectations for the constellation of short-term interest rates following liftoff have remained quite stable.	61	6
201509	118	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Responses to the Desk’s surveys and outreach to a wide range of counterparties, summarized in your middle-right panel, suggest that market participants view the Federal Reserve as having the necessary tools to control short- term interest rates following liftoff.	61	6
201509	119	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Specifically, the modal expectation is that the effective rate will print only slightly below the midpoint of a 25 to 50 basis point target range, and this expectation has been stable in responses to the Desk’s surveys for some time.	61	6
201509	120	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Other money market rates, with the exception of Treasury bills, are expected to trade at relatively similar spreads to the effective rate as they have over recent months.	61	6
201509	121	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	While respondents to the Desk’s surveys generally express confidence that the effective rate will print within the target range after liftoff—and assign very small probabilities to outcomes outside the range—they do assign a meaningful probability to it printing toward the bottom of that range.	61	6
201509	122	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	In particular, some commentary has highlighted that the effective lower bound may be providing support to money market rates that will recede as the target range is lifted.	61	6
201509	123	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Other commentary has focused on the risks that increased demand for short-term, high-quality assets related to SEC money fund reform and continued declines in dealer repo books will put downward pressure on repo rates and pull down the effective federal funds rate.	61	6
201509	124	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	More recently, market participants have noted that the timing of liftoff relative to the debt ceiling might also affect the path of money market rates.	61	6
201509	125	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Over the intermeeting period, Treasury Secretary Lew extended the debt issuance suspension period through October 30, which allows the Treasury to continue to use extraordinary measures to remain under the statutory debt limit while issuing new securities.	61	6
201509	126	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Staff and market projections suggest that these measures should allow the Treasury debt that is subject to the limit to stay below the statutory ceiling until around late November.	61	6
201509	127	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Staff projections, shown as the dotted light blue line in the bottom-right panel, suggest that bill supply could temporarily decline another $171 billion from current levels if the debt ceiling is not lifted in the near term.	61	6
201509	128	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	This reduction in supply could put downward pressure on money market rates, particularly for outstanding bills, and the potential combination of lower money market rates and reduced bill supply might prompt a sizable increase in demand at our reverse repo operations, particularly at quarter-end.	61	6
201509	129	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Demand for RRPs could be further boosted if, as in the past, money funds seek to avoid so-called at-risk securities or increase their investments in short-term, cash-like instruments in anticipation of possible redemptions.	61	6
201509	130	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	These pressures could increase the risk that demand at the ON RRP operations exceeds the current aggregate $300 billion limit.	61	6
201509	131	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	At this point, we have not seen evidence of concern about a possible disruptive debt ceiling outcome in financial markets.	61	6
201509	132	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	That said, in past episodes, and as shown in the top-left panel of your final exhibit, bill yields did not reflect such concern until closer to the date when the Treasury was expected to exhaust its extraordinary measures.	61	6
201509	133	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	As in previous episodes, the staff intends to prepare contingency plans for a variety of scenarios and will provide an update to the Committee at the October meeting or sooner, if needed.	61	6
201509	134	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Looking ahead, as detailed in a note sent to the Committee during the intermeeting period and outlined in the top-right panel, the Desk plans to offer term RRPs over the September quarter-end.	61	6
201509	135	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Should the Committee decide not to increase the target range, the Desk would propose offering $250 billion in term RRPs at 3 basis points over the ON RRP offered rate, apportioned between seven- and two- day maturities.	61	6
201509	136	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	This increased offering amount should mitigate, to some extent, the risk that a drop in Treasury bill supply might lead to more quarter-end pressure than we anticipate.	61	6
201509	137	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	This would be somewhat higher than the $200 billion offered over the past two quarter-end dates but below the $300 billion offered in December 2014.	61	6
201509	138	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	If the Committee decides to raise the target range at this meeting and directs the Desk to temporarily suspend the cap on the ON RRP, the Desk plans to continue to offer the already announced $200 billion in two term operations, also with seven- and two-day maturities, but at a 0 basis point spread to the ON RRP rate.	61	6
201509	139	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Without offering any financial incentive for investing cash for term instead of overnight, we would anticipate limited take-up in the term operations and larger take-up at the ON RRP operations.	61	6
201509	140	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Finally, I would like to provide a brief update on reinvestments.	61	6
201509	141	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	The Desk continues to reinvest principal payments on holdings of agency debt and MBS into agency MBS through secondary market purchases, and these operations proceeded smoothly over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201509	142	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Following our update at the July meeting, the Desk began aggregating agency MBS CUSIPs during the week of August 17, as planned.	61	6
201509	143	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	Over the period, there was some focus on the FOMC’s reinvestment policy following the discussion in the July FOMC meeting minutes.	61	6
201509	144	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	As in prior surveys, and shown in your middle-left panel, respondents view a phasing-out of Treasury security and agency MBS reinvestments as the most likely scenario and generally expect that reinvestments will be phased out over a 10- to 12-month period.	61	6
201509	145	2015-09-17	meeting	LOGAN	As shown in your middle-right panel, according to the Desk’s surveys, the median respondent expects that the FOMC will cease some or all Treasury security and agency MBS reinvestments 9 months and 8 months following liftoff, respectively.	61	6

201509	286	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	If I could just offer a little bit of additional information regarding President Bullard’s question about the relationship between commodity prices and exchange rates.	62	6
201509	287	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	We’ve done some research on this: basically, econometrically relating changes in currency values to the share of a country’s trade that’s in both oil and non-oil commodities.	62	6
201509	288	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	It turns out that if you run that regression over the entire past year, starting when the dollar started to fall, you actually get pretty precise, statistically significant estimates of the effect of both oil prices and non-oil commodity prices on exchange rates.	62	6
201509	289	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	That explains somewhere in the neighborhood of half of the cross-sectional dispersion of exchange rate depreciations.	62	6
201509	290	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	Now, if you take a snapshot of the previous intermeeting period, you might expect that, as it’s a more tumultuous time and a shorter period, you’d get a less precise reading on the relationship between commodity prices and exchange rates, and that is indeed what we found.	62	6
201509	291	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	You still have a statistically significant effect of oil prices on currencies, but it’s a smaller coefficient, and a not-statistically-significant effect of non-oil commodity prices on currencies.	62	6
201509	292	2015-09-17	meeting	KAMIN	That’s all to say that I think the basic relationship between commodity prices and a country’s exchange rate still holds, but it’s been obscured in this past month and a half with the more general market volatility.	62	6

201509	305	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	We’re next going to turn to “System Open Market Account Reinvestment Policy,” and I’m going to call on Beth Klee to start us off.	96	9
201509	306	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	307	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Chris Erceg and I will be referring to the materials titled “SOMA Reinvestment Policy.”	96	9
201509	308	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	At the July FOMC meeting, participants discussed various aspects of the decision to end reinvestment, including the appropriate economic conditionality of such a decision and the macroeconomic effects of alternative strategies.	96	9
201509	309	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	In that context, you recently received a follow-up memo that addressed a number of these questions.	96	9
201509	310	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	As noted in the upper panel of your first exhibit, key questions include the following: How much might the choice of reinvestment strategy matter for economic outcomes?	96	9
201509	311	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	How much might the choice of reinvestment strategy affect the path of the federal funds rate?	96	9
201509	312	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	And can reinvestment strategies that are linked to the federal funds rate improve economic outcomes in situations when the effective lower bound on the federal funds rate is binding?	96	9
201509	313	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	In our briefing, Chris and I will provide evidence on these issues and discuss possible implications for the reinvestment language options included in alternative C of the September Tealbook.	96	9
201509	314	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	To start, under the trigger strategies considered in the staff memo—and as noted in the lower panel—the Committee would announce that full reinvestment of the principal payments from Treasury and agency mortgage-backed securities would continue until the federal funds rate reaches the trigger—either 1 percent or 2 percent—and then stop permanently.	96	9
201509	315	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	These strategies have several key features.	96	9
201509	316	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	First, they are state dependent and, therefore, in line with the Policy Normalization Principles and Plans document, which indicates that the timing of the end of reinvestment will depend on the economic outlook.	96	9
201509	317	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Second, these strategies link reinvestment to the level of the policy rate, a feature that may be conceptually attractive as it then indirectly links the end of reinvestment to economic conditions.	96	9
201509	318	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Third, the conditionality is limited because the triggers, as constructed, imply that reinvestment ends permanently once the trigger is reached.	96	9
201509	319	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Of note, although we assume for illustrative purposes that the trigger strategies are geared toward specific numerical values of the funds rate, these strategies could be communicated through qualitative guidance about how far policy normalization had to proceed before reinvestment would be ended.	96	9
201509	320	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Your next exhibit explores some consequences of the trigger strategies if economic conditions evolve in line with the modal outlook.	96	9
201509	321	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Panel A compares the evolution of the SOMA portfolio under the baseline reinvestment strategy—for which it is assumed that reinvestment ceases roughly six months after liftoff, and which is depicted by the solid black line—with the trigger strategies.	96	9
201509	322	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	The 1 percent trigger— the blue dashed line—delays the end of reinvestment only briefly, while the 2 percent trigger—the red dotted line—delays the end of reinvestment until two years after liftoff, to late 2017.	96	9
201509	323	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	The larger balance sheet under each trigger strategy causes the term premium—panel B—to run a tad lower than under the baseline strategy.	96	9
201509	324	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Because the inertial Taylor rule assumed to govern the federal funds rate in our simulations does not raise policy rates enough to counteract this additional balance sheet stimulus, long-term real interest rates decline a touch compared with the baseline—panel C—and, consequently, the unemployment rate also falls a little more quickly—panel E.	96	9
201509	325	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	However, it is clear that the macroeconomic effects are small, even under the more accommodative 2 percent trigger.	96	9
201509	326	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	You might look at the results and wonder if reinvestment policy could matter much for macroeconomic outcomes.	96	9
201509	327	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Returning to the top of the exhibit, consider a polar case—shown by the green dash-dotted lines in panel A—in which the FOMC announces that the SOMA portfolio will be held constant at its current level for a very long time.	96	9
201509	328	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	In this case, the term premium drops about 40 basis points on the announcement—the green line in panel B—and the unemployment rate, panel E, falls about ¼ percentage point below baseline by 2018.	96	9
201509	329	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	This example highlights the result that reinvestment policy can have sizable effects, albeit under an extreme strategy.	96	9
201509	330	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Turning to your third exhibit, the stimulus from the alternative reinvestment strategies can be offset by a somewhat higher funds rate than implied by the inertial Taylor rule.	96	9
201509	331	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	As seen in panel A, we implement this offset for each strategy by boosting the intercept term of the Taylor rule by enough to achieve nearly the same macroeconomic outcomes as in the Tealbook baseline.	96	9
201509	332	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	As seen in panel B, only a small upward adjustment of the federal funds rate path is required under the trigger strategies—the blue and red lines—to mimic the baseline outcomes for unemployment, panel C, and inflation, panel D.	96	9
201509	333	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	By contrast, offsetting the effects of maintaining a constant portfolio requires a noticeably higher funds rate—the green line in panel B.	96	9
201509	334	2015-09-17	meeting	KLEE	Chris will now continue our briefing starting with your next exhibit.	96	9
201509	335	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Thank you, Beth.	96	9
201509	336	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Although the macroeconomic effects of the baseline and trigger reinvestment strategies do not differ greatly under the modal outlook, trigger strategies can provide considerably more balance sheet accommodation in response to adverse shocks, particularly those shocks that would drive the federal funds rate to the ELB.	96	9
201509	337	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	To illustrate this, in exhibit 4 we consider a recessionary shock that pins the federal funds rate at the ELB for three years under the baseline reinvestment strategy—the solid black line in panel A—and results in the unemployment rate—panel B—rising to 7½ percent.	96	9
201509	338	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Under the 2 percent trigger strategy, the red dotted line in panel A, the public expects that the federal funds rate will remain below 2  percent until 2021.	96	9
201509	339	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Accordingly, SOMA holdings, panel C, are expected to remain at their current level until the funds rate reaches 2 percent in 2021.	96	9
201509	340	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	The larger balance sheet under this strategy results in a much lower path for the term premium—the red line in panel D.	96	9
201509	341	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	As a result, the trigger strategy mitigates somewhat the rise in the unemployment rate—panel B—with the peak rise about 0.4 percentage point lower than under the baseline strategy.	96	9
201509	342	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	The bottom panel discusses three key implications of trigger strategies.	96	9
201509	343	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	First, the balance sheet stimulus under the trigger strategy in an adverse scenario comes from an expectations channel:  Because reinvestment is tied to the policy rate, which is expected to remain low for a long time, the balance sheet is expected to remain large for much longer than in the baseline.	96	9
201509	344	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Second—and relatedly—a “calendar based” strategy that simply delayed reinvestment for a couple of years after liftoff would provide much less economic stimulus in adverse scenarios.	96	9
201509	345	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	This outcome is illustrated by the strategy labeled “liftoff plus 2 years” shown by the dashed blue lines in panel C.	96	9
201509	346	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	It delays the end of reinvestment by two years after liftoff, to late 2017, and thus extends the SOMA holdings.	96	9
201509	347	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Because the effects on the term premium— panel D—are quite small, this alternative approach only slightly mitigates the rise in unemployment, the blue lines in panel B, relative to the baseline.	96	9
201509	348	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Returning to the bottom panel, the third key implication is that the trigger strategy only provides noticeable stimulus if the adverse shock occurs before the policy rate reaches the trigger.	96	9
201509	349	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	If the recessionary shocks occurred later, the trigger strategy— through delaying balance sheet adjustment a year or two—would provide only a small degree of additional stimulus.	96	9
201509	350	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	A strategy that would restart reinvestment in response to sufficiently bad shocks could potentially do better in limiting their adverse macroeconomic effects than would an irreversible trigger strategy, at least if this strategy were well understood by market participants.	96	9
201509	351	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Of course, the greater state dependence of this policy might make it more difficult to communicate.	96	9
201509	352	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	As noted in your final exhibit, paragraph 4 of alternative C provides two options to characterize the Committee’s thinking about when to cease or begin phasing out reinvestments.	96	9
201509	353	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	The first is to continue reinvesting “until normalization of the level of the federal funds rate is well under way,” and the second is to continue reinvesting “at least during the early stages of normalization of the level of the federal funds rate.”	96	9
201509	354	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	You might view “well under way” as qualitatively consistent with the trigger strategies discussed in the memo.	96	9
201509	355	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	In particular, market participants might interpret this language as suggesting that reinvestment would not cease until economic conditions had led the Committee to increase the target range for the federal funds rate more than a few times.	96	9
201509	356	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	In addition, the public might conclude that, in the event that the economic outlook deteriorated, reinvestment could continue for a long time.	96	9
201509	357	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	This qualitative language would leave some ambiguity about how high the federal funds rate would have to rise to trigger an end of reinvestment, although policymakers could help clarify the public’s understanding through speeches, testimony, and other communications.	96	9
201509	358	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	In contrast, “at least during the early stages” might be interpreted as indicating that the Committee intends to cease or phase out reinvestment after a few hikes in the policy rate even if the federal funds rate stayed relatively low thereafter.	96	9
201509	359	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Market participants would probably view this language as indicating that policymakers place a relatively high priority on beginning to normalize the size of the balance sheet fairly soon.	96	9
201509	360	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	However, the qualification “at least” would likely suggest that the Committee wanted to retain some option to continue reinvestments if the labor market was to weaken or if inflation was stubbornly low.	96	9
201509	361	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Thank you very much.	96	9
201509	362	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Beth and I will be happy to take your questions.	96	9

201509	399	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	The amount of government debt in the hands of the public is lower as long as we’re holding a portfolio of a given size, and that’s what the nonneutrality is—it’s on the size of the government debt, and that’s a particular monetary policy that has been undertaken in a way that does, in fact, make a change in the government debt, and it’s nonneutral.	96	9
201509	400	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Chris.	96	9
201509	401	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	I just wanted to add that it might be helpful to think of the balance sheet policy as essentially affecting what we might call the equilibrium real rate or the natural real rate.	96	9
201509	402	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	From that perspective, balance sheet policies that depress term premiums very persistently would in essence be tantamount to raising the natural real rate.	96	9
201509	403	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	And that’s essentially what’s happening in these exercises, and it would be underscored in exhibit 3, in which you see the increase in the federal funds rate at relatively long horizons.	96	9
201509	404	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	Now, of course, this effect is derived from an estimated model.	96	9

201509	406	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	It’s consistent with relatively large and persistent effects on term premiums associated with very protracted changes in the level of long-term assets on the Federal Reserve’s balance sheet.	96	9
201509	407	2015-09-17	meeting	ERCEG	So that drives these very persistent effects, but it’s certainly based on an estimated model over the pre-crisis period.	96	9

201509	413	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And so the change in the composition of the assets in the hands of the public lowers the term premium below what it would be otherwise in a permanent fashion, for a given particular time path of public debt issuance by the Treasury.	96	9
201509	414	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	Show me a model.	96	9

201509	418	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Thomas.	96	9
201509	419	2015-09-17	meeting	LAUBACH	If I may just quickly elaborate on some of the things that President Kocherlakota and also Chris mentioned.	96	9

201509	421	2015-09-17	meeting	LAUBACH	If you didn’t accommodate that or didn’t take that into consideration in your policy rule by moving the intercept appropriately, you would end up missing your inflation target permanently.	96	9
201509	422	2015-09-17	meeting	LAUBACH	Now, the policy rule has, of course, the 2 percent inflation target hardwired into it, so it would constantly push against the resulting inflation overshoot that would result from not adjusting the intercept of the policy rule.	96	9

201509	476	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	477	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	I have a brief comment.	96	9
201509	478	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	First, I very much appreciate the staff memo.	96	9
201509	479	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	I thought it framed the issues very well—very small effects when using the modal forecast scenario, but more significant effects in terms of insuring against adverse outcomes.	96	9
201509	480	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	In other words, reducing the probability of returning to the zero lower bound, which I think is the key issue that we’re really talking about here.	96	9
201509	481	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	In terms of the language in alternative C, I think we need a small addition.	96	9
201509	482	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	I’m fine with the phrase “until normalization of the level of the federal funds rate is well under way,” but I think we should add an additional condition that we think that the normalization is expected to prove sustainable or something similar.	96	9
201509	483	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	Paragraph 5 in alternative C might read something like “The Committee anticipates doing so until the normalization of the level of funds rate is well under way and is expected to prove sustainable.”	96	9
201509	484	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	The notion I have in mind here is that the end of reinvestment should not be tied mechanically to the level of the funds rate but also tied to the economic outlook.	96	9
201509	485	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	I could imagine supporting an early end of reinvestment at a relatively low federal funds rate if the economy was forecast to grow very rapidly over the following year with very few downside risks in terms of the economic outlook.	96	9
201509	486	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	In that case, the likelihood would be that the Committee would raise the federal funds rate further over the next year, and the probability of an early return to the effective lower bound would be very low.	96	9
201509	487	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	In contrast, I’d favor waiting to end reinvestments if the economy was lacking forward momentum even at a somewhat higher federal funds rate.	96	9
201509	488	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s because the risk of returning to the effective lower bound would be higher in that case.	96	9
201509	489	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	The point I’m trying to make is that we’re delaying reinvestment for a reason—to reduce the probability of returning to the effective lower bound.	96	9
201509	490	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	That should really be the parameter that we’re focusing on.	96	9
201509	491	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	That probability is not just governed by the level of the federal funds rate, but also the economic outlook at the time.	96	9
201509	492	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	We want to end reinvestments when that probability is relatively low, and I think that’s not sufficiently summed up just by “well advanced.”	96	9
201509	493	2015-09-17	meeting	DUDLEY	I think you have to get the concept of the outlook in there as well.	96	9
201509	494	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	96	9
201509	495	2015-09-17	meeting	YELLEN	President Williams.	96	9
201509	496	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	497	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’m glad to say I’m comfortable with either version of the reinvestment language in alternative C and most other aspects of alternative C as well.	96	9
201509	498	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Oh, but I’ll leave that to tomorrow’s discussion.	96	9
201509	499	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	[Laughter]	96	9
201509	500	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I thought you might appreciate that.	96	9
201509	501	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’d like to make a few observations about the staff memo, quite honestly picking up on most of the themes that were discussed in response to some of the questions and also Vice Chairman Dudley’s comments.	96	9
201509	502	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	In terms of the staff memo, making decisions about the appropriate size of the balance sheet, I think it’s very important that we weigh both the costs and the benefits under a wide range of plausible scenarios.	96	9
201509	503	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	The memo emphasized one specific adverse scenario in order to illustrate a potential benefit of using a state-dependent rule for any reinvestments.	96	9
201509	504	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	As was described in the memo and in the presentation, in this particular scenario, a state-dependent rule continues reinvestment for several years beyond the baseline, and the larger resulting balance sheet provides additional stimulus relative to the case of ending reinvestments on a preset path.	96	9
201509	505	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	But in considering the merits of such a state-dependent rule, we shouldn’t lose sight of the bigger issue of the relative costs and benefits of maintaining a very large balance sheet.	96	9
201509	506	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Indeed, taking the analysis of the memo to its logical extreme, we should always maintain a very large balance sheet, arguably even larger than what we have today, to provide insurance against negative shocks.	96	9
201509	507	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I’m picking up on something implied by Vice Chairman Dudley’s comment because, according to the Board analysis we discussed, a large SOMA portfolio lowers the term premium and effectively shifts the IS curve out, and this implies a higher equilibrium federal funds rate—it’s about ½ percentage point higher than baseline for the constant portfolio.	96	9
201509	508	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	What does that do?	96	9
201509	509	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	It provides us with more buffer to use the federal funds rate as a policy instrument to respond to negative shocks.	96	9
201509	510	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I see this memo not really being about this scenario or about the strategy for ending reinvestments.	96	9
201509	511	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Really, it’s a question of, fundamentally, do we need a bigger buffer, especially in light of concerns about a continuing very low equilibrium real interest rate?	96	9
201509	512	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	In a way, I think the memo narrows the question too much to just the specific language concerning how we would end reinvestments, and we really should think much more carefully about both the benefits more generally of having a large balance sheet, and also revisit the costs around that because the memo obviously can only cover the benefits.	96	9
201509	513	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, we’ve decided through our discussions in the past that the costs of holding a very large balance sheet outweigh the benefits at most times, and we shouldn’t lose track of that in thinking through some of these issues in terms of the reinvestment strategy, as opposed to just focusing on the benefits under certain scenarios.	96	9
201509	514	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I recognize I am veering into the topic of the long-run policy strategy that the Chair has initiated and is under way, but I do think this is an important issue to think about.	96	9
201509	515	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	If we really do think we’re in a world of a lower equilibrium real rate and we are worried about downside risks, I think we really should be thinking in very broad terms about what the costs and benefits of the balance sheet are.	96	9
201509	516	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	And getting back to President Bullard’s question, what do we really understand about the effects of the balance sheet on the equilibrium real interest rate, and what are these tradeoffs that we’re facing?	96	9
201509	517	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think that the memo is fine—I have no quibbles about the memo—but it narrows this discussion down far too much.	96	9
201509	518	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	The discussion we need to be having is about this broader issue of the costs and benefits of the balance sheet and not just thinking through this one experiment because, more generally, in a stochastic environment, we’re going to get hit by both positive and negative shocks.	96	9
201509	519	2015-09-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Again, I’m looking forward to the work that’s being started in the work group with regard to the long-run policy initiative, but I do hope that this is one of the issues on which they give us better guidance, and sooner rather than later.	96	9

201509	524	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	525	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	We’ve had questions from President Bullard and discussions already by Vice Chairman Dudley and President Williams.	96	9
201509	526	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	There’s a very interesting issue present in this discussion, which is:  How should we think of the influence of the balance sheet?	96	9
201509	527	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	And what this analysis has brought out has been that, essentially, in the “stock” view of what it is that quantitative easing does, all of the quantitative easing that has been done so far is in the balance sheet—it’s there and it’s having an effect.	96	9
201509	528	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Let me ask the next question and then I’ll tell you the effect.	96	9
201509	529	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	When we do quantitative easing, the short rate remains unchanged.	96	9
201509	530	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Well, what happens to other interest rates?	96	9
201509	531	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Our analysis is that quantitative easing goes into the term premium.	96	9
201509	532	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	We buy up assets, and the money that’s put into the system goes into longer-term assets and reduces the term premium.	96	9
201509	533	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	So the next question is, what do we want to do over the longer term, after we’ve normalized the federal funds rate?	96	9
201509	534	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	If we had already reduced the balance sheet to its steady-state level, we might have a lower federal funds rate.	96	9
201509	535	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	At some point, we might even reach the point at which we would be at a near-zero federal funds rate again.	96	9
201509	536	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	What the large balance sheet is doing for us is keeping us away from the effective lower bound, as both Vice Chairman Dudley and President Williams pointed out, and it’s providing us with a twist in the term structure which we probably like.	96	9
201509	537	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	We haven’t really seen the effects of quantitative easing show up in investment spending very much, but, in principle, it is a factor encouraging investment.	96	9
201509	538	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	And the question is: What do we want to do?	96	9
201509	539	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Do we want to keep this effect of having a higher federal funds rate and a lower longer-term rate?	96	9
201509	540	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	We’d like the possibility of keeping the federal funds rate positive for longer, I assume, because we don’t like what we’d have to do if we got back down to zero, and it will be politically controversial and so forth.	96	9
201509	541	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	So it’s useful to have this “stock of QE” under our control.	96	9
201509	542	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	We can reduce it at some time by stopping reinvestment, and we could in effect reduce the stock of QE under our control.	96	9
201509	543	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	So this is a kind of investment and also a monetary policy choice, in terms of what term structure we are looking at.	96	9
201509	544	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	What this analysis did in my case was to make me think better of holding the larger portfolio for longer because then it would be safer for us and for the economy in terms of keeping us away from the zero lower bound in the event that we get a lot of negative shocks.	96	9
201509	545	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Well, we may get them, we may not get them, but they’re a risk, and we’d prefer not to be there.	96	9
201509	546	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	So I don’t know which of these strategies I’d like more.	96	9
201509	547	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	I think we should look at the likely quantitative effects of the two approaches.	96	9
201509	548	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	I think that what the analysis does show, particularly with that green line which goes out there forever, is that the effect of QE is 40 basis points on the federal funds rate—that’s what our models say, precisely.	96	9
201509	549	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	I conclude that we ought to rethink and keep reinvestment going a little longer.	96	9
201509	550	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	We have a slight problem in the fact that when you read what people say, they all assume it’s six months after normalization begins before we end reinvestment.	96	9
201509	551	2015-09-17	meeting	FISCHER	Simon, I must get a different set of analyst remarks from that you get, but that’s what I find in the analyst remarks that I read—six months, nine months—and I think we might want to go longer.	96	9

201509	578	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	Overall, I want to think of the FOMC as just choosing the level of accommodation.	96	9
201509	579	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	That’s maybe just a better way to think about it.	96	9
201509	580	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	Accommodation can be provided through two channels—either through the federal funds rate or through a balance sheet policy action.	96	9
201509	581	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	In the first set of simulations, the FOMC acts by following an inertial Taylor rule.	96	9
201509	582	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	In reality, the Committee chooses how much monetary accommodation to provide based on macroeconomic circumstances.	96	9
201509	583	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	So the effects of a particular reinvestment strategy of the type outlined in the memo could be completely offset by an appropriate choice of the federal funds rate path, as has been discussed in the previous comments.	96	9
201509	584	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	This is illustrated in the memo.	96	9
201509	585	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	So what’s the meaning of different reinvestment strategies and probably different levels of accommodation?	96	9
201509	586	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	We want to leave it up to the Committee to change policy based on whatever the Committee thinks.	96	9
201509	587	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	And surely we want that deliberation to go on, and we want the Committee to be looking at the right level of accommodation at a particular point in time.	96	9
201509	588	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	So I’m not sure it’s a useful exercise, the way it’s constructed in the memo.	96	9
201509	589	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	And as far as our policy is concerned, I think we might as well stick to the baseline strategy of ending reinvestments six months after liftoff, and then go ahead and let the Committee choose the federal funds rate path that it judges provides the appropriate level of accommodation.	96	9
201509	590	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	In the second exercise in the memo—reinvestment strategies under the adverse scenario—a similar conclusion can be drawn.	96	9
201509	591	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	In this scenario, the economy is forced back to the zero lower bound in 2017 for three years.	96	9
201509	592	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	A trigger-type reinvestment strategy could provide accommodation in this scenario, but of course the Committee can simply choose the appropriate level of accommodation by choosing the appropriate level of the balance sheet—that is, presumably by reinstituting QE.	96	9
201509	593	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	I know you’re swearing off QE, but if you get in the right circumstance, you’re going to use the tool again.	96	9
201509	594	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	The FOMC could get exactly the right level of accommodation and not have to rely on the approximate amount offered by the trigger strategy.	96	9
201509	595	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	So why are we going through this elaborate exercise of having the trigger strategy, and then we’re going to adjust our path of the funds rate that’s going to give the right level of accommodation, taking into account what the trigger strategy is going to do over the forecast horizon?	96	9
201509	596	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	I’m not sure that this is that useful of a way to think about it.	96	9
201509	597	2015-09-17	meeting	BULLARD	It’s due to the substitutability of the two approaches to providing monetary accommodation.	96	9

201509	644	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	645	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s been a very rich discussion already.	96	9
201509	646	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I have to admit that I was pretty surprised at the July meeting when it became apparent that more than a few FOMC participants viewed halting reinvestments as being a decision that would be very costly to reverse by reinitiating purchases or by beginning reinvestments again, as President Mester suggested.	96	9
201509	647	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think this lack of reversibility is pretty crucial in terms of how we think about the appropriate long-run size of the balance sheet, the matter President Williams raised.	96	9
201509	648	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If, as President Bullard and President Mester suggested, we’ll just turn on this tool when we need it and, if we hit the effective lower bound and we need more accommodation, we’ll begin reinvestments again or we’ll begin active purchases again, then I think it’s a very different question than if we view halting reinvestments as having enormous costs associated with it.	96	9
201509	649	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And then we have to weigh the political economy—assuming that those costs are coming from political economy considerations—against the political economy considerations that President Lacker pointed to, which are also very real in maintaining a large balance sheet.	96	9
201509	650	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These are very, very challenging issues.	96	9
201509	651	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we take as given this lack of reversibility—and that’s the way the memo drafted the problem and, in light of what I heard at the July meeting, I am very sympathetic to that way of drafting the memo—this irreversibility leads to worse economic outcomes if we have to return to the effective lower bound.	96	9
201509	652	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I argued at the July meeting, to me this means we should only be willing to halt reinvestments at a point in time when we’re willing to increase the effective lower bound on interest rates.	96	9
201509	653	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Basically, halting reinvestments is taking a tool away from yourself.	96	9
201509	654	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	You should only be willing to do that if you are actually willing to take away another tool, which is to increase the effective lower bound on interest rates.	96	9
201509	655	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My own thinking is, in light of where I think the long-run neutral real rate is going to be and given our recent experience, I’m pretty loath to throw away these kinds of tools at this stage.	96	9
201509	656	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My preference at this point would be to leave out any reference to reinvestment policy at the time of liftoff.	96	9
201509	657	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I thought President Kaplan raised some great points, too, about the learning that’s going to occur.	96	9
201509	658	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	He hasn’t been involved in these conversations before, so he hasn’t gotten totally into the “inside the tent” kind of thinking that we all have about this, and I think that’s good.	96	9
201509	659	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think that having an opportunity to learn about how this process is going to work before starting to talk about a new thing that we aren’t sure how it is going to work is wise counsel.	96	9
201509	660	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	At the time of liftoff, the Chair could say that this matter remains under study by the staff and principals.	96	9
201509	661	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it allows us an opportunity to dovetail our thinking about reinvestments with the longer-run operating framework exercise that the Chair initiated.	96	9
201509	662	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One comment on the normalization principles.	96	9
201509	663	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The normalization principles certainly have indicated that the Committee likes having a smaller balance sheet.	96	9
201509	664	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On the other hand, the normalization principles have also said we want to keep as large a balance sheet as is necessary to be able to conduct effective monetary policy.	96	9
201509	665	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That could be a lot larger than we thought it was going to be three or four years ago—it might be larger than we think it is today.	96	9
201509	666	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think this Committee will have to engage in some serious conversations about that.	96	9
201509	667	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, assuming the Committee does want to make a reference to reinvestments, I’m concerned about the vagueness of what’s in the draft of alternative C.	96	9
201509	668	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If Committee members can agree among themselves to the idea of tying reinvestments to something specific in the outlook, something specific regarding the federal funds rate, as was done in the memo, it would be really great to offer that kind of clarity.	96	9
201509	669	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	President Evans was chastising me for smiling at Chris’s suggestion that we rely on the speeches of the presidents to elucidate what the Committee means by this language.	96	9
201509	670	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The intermeeting period has not given me the kind of confidence that maybe I would like to have with that as a communications device.	96	9
201509	671	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	[Laughter]	96	9
201509	672	2015-09-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I’ve indicated before, I think if we can agree on a tie to something like the federal funds rate, it would be really good to be able to make that connection as opposed to relying on everyone’s interpretation of what that might mean.	96	9

201509	692	2015-09-17	meeting	TARULLO	As people probably don’t recall, but I’ll remind you, I raised a couple of things that might happen—which I don’t expect to happen, but which might happen—in the early stages after liftoff.	96	9

201509	705	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	706	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I have a strong desire, which I assume is widely shared, to avoid returning to the effective lower bound.	96	9
201509	707	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I also suspect that the possibility of doing so is uncomfortably high.	96	9
201509	708	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	On gearing up quantitative easing again, I want to say a couple of things.	96	9
201509	709	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	First, I think we’ve never looked at asset purchases as other than a second-best tool.	96	9
201509	710	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I think that’s been the way it’s been talked about since the very beginning—uncertain as to its effect, uncertain as to bad effects, and certainly uncertain as to political economy characteristics.	96	9
201509	711	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I would put a decision to end reinvestments and then a decision to reverse that as not at all akin to a decision to start asset purchases again.	96	9
201509	712	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	To me, they’re two completely different things.	96	9
201509	713	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I think if the Committee were to decide to end reinvestments and then six months later there were a shock, I think it would be an easy decision to begin reinvestments again on the grounds that we would have so little to work with in the way of rate cuts to make.	96	9
201509	714	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	So I look at those decisions as very different.	96	9
201509	715	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	Those things lead me to a strong preference for the “until normalization of the level of the federal funds rate is well under way.”	96	9
201509	716	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I could learn to like the 1 percent or 2 percent thresholds.	96	9
201509	717	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I will say that the real economy effects are tiny, and, if I’m right they would be even smaller if, in fact, the irreversibility assumption is really not right as a practical matter.	96	9
201509	718	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	But I’m open to learning more about those.	96	9
201509	719	2015-09-17	meeting	POWELL	I’d prefer to work with interest rates until we get way away from the effective lower bound.	96	9

201509	723	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201509	724	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	My judgment is animated by, first, a desire to maintain the parsimony and clarity of our policy framework and, second, by what I believe to be asymmetric risk-management considerations over the period that we are considering.	96	9
201509	725	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	Those considerations lead me to favor a 2 percent quantitative target.	96	9
201509	726	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	The policy framework that we’ve agreed to over the past year makes the federal funds rate the focal point for our policy stance.	96	9
201509	727	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	It’s clear, it’s familiar to market participants, and it’s empirically well understood, and I’d like to keep the focus on that.	96	9
201509	728	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	In addition, we’ve spent considerable effort over the past several meetings trying to make the public understand that our policy framework is based on the economic outlook and not on a calendar.	96	9
201509	729	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	I’d be very reluctant to have our reinvestment policy in any way migrate back toward a time-based framework.	96	9
201509	730	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	I think the most parsimonious way to capture economic developments is to condition our reinvestment policy very clearly on the behavior of the one variable that we have focused on, which is the federal funds rate.	96	9
201509	731	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	It summarizes information from a wide variety of economic indicators on progress toward our goals.	96	9
201509	732	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	With regard to risk-management considerations, I think the staff analysis did a very nice job of laying those out.	96	9
201509	733	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	Like others, I don’t believe the bar for reinitiating QE is at all similar to a bar for a 25 basis point cut, for instance, or turning on and off reinvestment.	96	9
201509	734	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	So I do believe that that effective lower bound constraint is a very important one.	96	9
201509	735	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	For all those reasons, I would like to see the reinvestment conditioned on a 2 percent rate.	96	9
201509	736	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	I think it’s clear, it gives us that cushion, and we can easily avoid the notion that you turn off reinvestment permanently once you’ve hit 2 percent by making clear that only while you’re above 2 percent would reinvestment cease.	96	9
201509	737	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	I think that would be pretty easy to introduce into the language.	96	9
201509	738	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	That would be my strong preference.	96	9
201509	739	2015-09-17	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
